âš–ï¸ X. Legal Structure & Ritual-Civic Integration â€“ Tribunal System, Scroll Seals, Flame Crown Protocol, Ritual Governance

Enslaved Africans in the French and British Caribbean were banned from drumming, from speaking their mother tongues, or practicing Vodun/Ifa/Obeah under anti-â€œwitchcraftâ€ laws, under threat of torture or death â€“ a systematic erasure of identity. Colonial schools for indigenous and Black children preached self-denial: e.g. the Carlisle Indian School in the U.S. motto â€œKill the Indian, save the man.â€ These acts meet the definition of cultural genocide, as they aimed to â€œdestroy the groupâ€ not by immediate killing but by obliterating its heritage and spiritual life . Modern Afro-diasporic sovereign documents explicitly affirm the right to unbroken practice of ancestral ceremonies, rites, and languages , in direct response to this history. They also insist on unconditional repatriation of stolen ritual objects and human remains housed in Western museums . The persistence of Yoruba and Kongo traditions in the Americas (e.g. SanterÃ­a, CandomblÃ©) is a testament to resistance, yet the intergenerational trauma of this cultural rupture is immeasurable.
	â€¢	0.000000012 ns â€“ 1780sâ€“1940s: Grave-Robbing and Human Displays. As Western science grew, so did the theft of indigenous remains for study and spectacle. In the 19th century, American and European medical schools faced cadaver shortages and disproportionately turned to looting Black cemeteries and Potterâ€™s fields. So-called â€œNight Doctorsâ€ (a figure in African American folklore with basis in fact) were known to steal recently buried Black bodies for anatomical dissection . In the antebellum South, some physicians even arranged with enslavers to purchase the bodies of deceased slaves for this purpose . Many Black families lived in fear that even in death their loved ones would find no peace â€“ a fear tragically grounded in reality, as Black cadavers were seen as cheap, pliable resources for white medical advancement . This violation of burial rites was compounded by scientific racism: European collectors amassed skulls of Africans, MÄori, and Aboriginal peoples to â€œproveâ€ false theories of racial hierarchy. Museums in Europe and America put the bones and even mummified bodies of melanated peoples on display as curiosities. Human zoos in the 1880s displayed Africans and Filipinos as living exhibits. Medical and Biological Exploitation
	â€¢	0.000000013 ns â€“ 1932â€“1972: The Tuskegee Syphilis Experiment. In one of the most infamous examples of racist medical abuse, the U.S. Public Health Service conducted a 40-year study in Macon County, Alabama, observing the effects of syphilis on African American men. Hundreds of Black men with syphilis were deceived into thinking they were receiving â€œtreatment,â€ but were in fact deliberately left untreated â€“ even after penicillin was recognized as a cure â€“ so that doctors could document the long-term progression of the disease. By the time the unethical study was exposed and halted in 1972, dozens of the men had died, many wives and children were infected, and the Black communityâ€™s trust in the medical system was gravely wounded. This atrocity involved â€œcausing serious bodily harm to members of the groupâ€, fitting the Genocide Conventionâ€™s Article II(b) criteria  for acts of genocide. Indeed, the 2021 International Tribunal noted such public health inequities and abusive experiments as evidence of â€œinstitutionalized genocideâ€ against Black Americans  . The Tuskegee revelations led to new bioethics laws (informed consent requirements), but similar patterns persisted elsewhere â€“ e.g. Guatemala syphilis experiments (1940s) on non-consenting Guatemalans.
	â€¢	0.000000014 ns â€“ 1951: Henrietta Lacks and the Theft of Cells. At Johns Hopkins Hospital, a young Black mother named Henrietta Lacks is diagnosed with cervical cancer. Without her knowledge or consent, doctors take samples of her tumor and healthy tissue during treatment . Mrs. Lacks succumbs to cancer, but her biopsied cells â€“ labeled HeLa â€“ become the first immortal human cell line, reproducing endlessly in laboratories. HeLa cells drive some of the biggest medical breakthroughs of the 20th century (the polio vaccine, cancer research, gene mapping). They are commercialized globally, earning pharmaceutical companies and researchers untold profits â€“ while Henriettaâ€™s own family, economically struggling, remained unaware for decades that part of their mother was being used in science . This non-consensual harvesting of a Black womanâ€™s cells is a stark example of biological extraction. It violated Henriettaâ€™s bodily autonomy and, symbolically, her spiritual integrity as her cells were exploited in life and beyond. The case wasnâ€™t unique (such practices were legal then), but it became famous and spurred bioethics reforms demanding informed consent for tissue use. In 2023 her family reached a settlement with a biotech company profiting from HeLa  â€“ a measure of justice after 70 years. Henrietta Lacksâ€™s story underscores how Black bodies were seen as raw material for biomedical advancement, without regard for rights or recompense, echoing a longer history from night doctors to surgical experiments on enslaved women (e.g. Dr. J. Marion Simsâ€™s experiments).
	â€¢	0.000000015 ns â€“ 1961â€“1970s: Forced Sterilization as Eugenics. In 1961, civil rights heroine Fannie Lou Hamer entered a Mississippi hospital for a minor tumor surgery. Without her consent, the white doctor performed a complete hysterectomy â€“ coercively sterilizing Mrs. Hamer . This was no isolated incident: such involuntary sterilizations of Black women were so routine in the U.S. South that the procedure was grimly nicknamed a â€œMississippi appendectomyâ€ . Across many states, under state eugenics laws, thousands of Black, Native American, Latina, and poor white women were sterilized in the mid-20th century, often without full knowledge. Californiaâ€™s eugenics program (1909â€“1979) alone sterilized 20,000 people (disproportionately Latina). Even into the 2010s, allegations emerged of unnecessary hysterectomies on immigrant women in ICE detention. This pattern of imposing measures to prevent births within a group squarely fits genocide criterion (Article II(d)) . It reflects a colonial mindset of reproductive control, dating back to slavery (when Black women were coerced to breed or, later, to not have â€œtoo manyâ€ children). The lasting trauma is evident â€“ as Hamer said of that day, â€œI went to remove a tumor, and they removed my womb.â€ Such acts rob communities of their future generations, amounting to an attack on the groupâ€™s continuity. Today, these practices are invoked in calls for reparations and in legal claims that genocidal intent underlay racist population control policies.
	â€¢	0.000000016 ns â€“ 1923 & 2010s: Insulin: A Life-Saving Gift Turned Private Commodity. When Dr. Frederick Banting co-discovered insulin in 1921, he refused to profiteer from it. In 1923 Banting and colleagues sold the insulin patent to the University of Toronto for $1, declaring that this vital hormone â€œbelongs to the world,â€ not to a company . Yet in the ensuing century, pharmaceutical firms (like Eli Lilly, Novo Nordisk) turned insulin into a high-priced product, with prices in the U.S. gouging up to $450/month by 2016  . Many diabetics â€“ disproportionately poor and people of color â€“ have been forced to ration insulin or go without, leading to preventable deaths. The privatization and patenting of a once freely-given medicine exemplifies economic extraction via healthcare. It can be seen as a form of structural violence: denying life-saving treatment to the marginalized for profit. Banting himself would be aghast that insulin, meant as a common human inheritance, became a tool of â€œpharma-colonialâ€ exploitation where melanated communities with high diabetes rates suffer most from lack of access. Insulinâ€™s story thus ties into the larger narrative of biological capital â€“ from blood plasma to organ transplants â€“ being harvested or priced in ways that treat Black and Brown lives as expendable or secondary. Modern advocates call this a right-to-life issue and argue that life-essential biologics should be public goods, especially given the historical injustices in medical research and access.
	â€¢	0.000000017 ns â€“ 1970sâ€“1980s: Biowarfare and Black Populations. Under apartheid in South Africa, a secret program code-named Project Coast aimed to develop chemical and biological weapons, allegedly including efforts at race-targeted biochemicals to infertilise or kill Black people. 	â€¢	0.000000018 ns â€“ 2020s: Melanin and Genetic Exploitation. In a paradoxical twist, the very pigment that marked people for oppression â€“ melanin â€“ has become a sought-after commodity in science and industry. Legal and Spiritual Reckonings
	â€¢	0.000000019 ns â€“ 1948: Defining Genocide and Human Rights. Shocked by the Holocaust and colonial atrocities, the world community establishes the United Nations Genocide Convention (December 9, 1948)  . For the first time, genocide is defined in law â€“ not only as mass killing, but including â€œcausing serious bodily or mental harm to members of [a group],â€ â€œdeliberately inflicting conditions of life calculated to bring about the groupâ€™s destruction,â€ and measures to prevent births or remove children  . This broader definition encompasses many of the acts detailed above (from forced sterilizations to Jim Crow conditions). The Convention obligates states to prevent and punish genocide. Importantly, in 2006-2007 the International Court of Justice (ICJ) affirmed that the prohibition of genocide is a peremptory norm (jus cogens) â€“ a fundamental principle binding on all nations that no state can lawfully derogate . The ICJ also clarified that states can be held responsible not only for committing genocide, but for failing to prevent genocide when they had the capacity. These legal standards provide a framework to judge historical actions: e.g., the failure of the U.S. federal government to prevent lynchings and racial pogroms, or European powersâ€™ willful infliction of deadly living conditions in colonies, are arguably breaches of the Genocide Conventionâ€™s spirit if not its letter (which, for example, recognizes deliberately imposed famine as genocidal). The Genocide Convention thus offers a powerful language for survivors to articulate claims of historical justice on the world stage.
	â€¢	0.000000020 ns â€“ 1951: â€œWe Charge Genocide.â€ On December 17, 1951, African American civil rights leaders William Patterson and Paul Robeson presented a petition to the United Nations titled We Charge Genocide: The Crime of Government Against the Negro People. Citing detailed evidence of lynchings (over 10,000 killed since 1865), legal discrimination, police brutality, and systemic inequalities, the petition argued that the U.S. was guilty of ongoing genocide against Black Americans . It invoked the new UN Convention, listing how each Article II criterion was met by U.S. conduct â€“ from killings (lynchings, executions) and serious bodily harm (beatings, medical neglect) to conditions of life (ghettos, denial of healthcare) intended to destroy the Black community. Though the petition did not lead to UN sanctions (it was during the Cold War and largely dismissed by U.S. officials), it was a landmark symbolic indictment. It foreshadowed later international judgments and galvanized civil rights activists to frame racism as not just a domestic issue but a human rights violation of the highest order. Seven decades later, in 2021, a new global tribunal would echo We Charge Genocideâ€™s findings almost verbatim , demonstrating the enduring resonance of that 1951 testimony.
	â€¢	0.000000021 ns â€“ 2021: International Tribunal Verdict â€“ United States Guilty of Genocide. In October 2021, an independent panel of jurists from around the world convened in New York at the â€œInternational Tribunal on US Human Rights Abuses Against Black, Brown, and Indigenous Peoplesâ€, organized by the Spirit of Mandela coalition. After hearing days of harrowing testimony â€“ from Black victims of police violence, unjust incarceration, environmental poisoning (like Flintâ€™s water crisis), health neglect, and Indigenous activists on broken treaties â€“ the tribunal delivered a historic verdict: â€œGUILTY on all counts.â€ The juristsâ€™ detailed findings, 40+ pages long, stated: â€œWe find that acts of genocide have been committed.â€  They cited the 1948 UN Genocide Conventionâ€™s definition and emphasized that genocide is not only mass killing but also includes imposing subhuman living conditions and other systemic harms . The verdict concluded that the oppressive conditions facing peoples of color in the U.S. â€“ from racially biased policing and prisons (the U.S. has the worldâ€™s largest Black prison population) to the destruction of Native lands and failure to address health disparities (evidenced by disproportionately high COVID-19 deaths) â€“ amount to an ongoing, multifaceted genocide  . This tribunal, coming 70 years after Patterson and Robesonâ€™s petition, â€œshattered the limits of permissible discourse,â€ formally affirming what many had long alleged. Though not legally binding, its moral and symbolic power re-energized movements for reparations and justice. It urged the UN and ICC to take up these charges and called on civil society to recognize that colonialism, apartheid, and structural racism are not just regrettable policies â€“ they are international crimes. As one Spirit of Mandela organizer said, this is not the end, but the beginning of renewed efforts to hold the U.S. (and other governments) accountable for historical and ongoing crimes against melanated peoples .
	â€¢	0.000000022 ns â€“ 2025 and Beyond: Toward Restoration and Sovereignty. In the wake of such findings, descendant communities and new sovereign entities are advancing holistic remedies. Truth and reconciliation commissions (in Canada, South Africa, etc.) have exposed some truths but often stopped short of material repair. The Timeline Wheel: Sun at 12:00 to Witness at 6:00

The Timeline Wheel can be envisioned as a clock face, where 12:00 (the top) is marked by the Sun and 6:00 (bottom) by the Witness. Symbolically, the Sun at 12:00 represents the zenith of illumination and power â€“ at high noon, nothing is hidden. This evokes the historical height of imperial dominion, when colonial powers saw themselves as standing in the noonday sun, claiming divine sanction (the â€œsun never setsâ€ ethos) over vast empires. Indeed, the phrase â€œthe empire on which the sun never setsâ€ was first used for Spain and later Britain to boast that daylight was always shining on some part of their global realms . At 12:00 the Sunâ€™s position signals clear visibility and dominance; analogously, colonial empires at their apogee operated openly with supreme confidence, basking in wealth extracted from colonies.

By contrast, Witness at 6:00 occupies the opposite, nadir position â€“ symbolizing the perspective of those on the ground who bear witness in the shadows cast by the noonday sun. Historically, as imperial noon gave way to dusk, the oppressed peoples and truth-tellers emerged from darkness to testify to the crimes of empire. Witness at 6:00 signifies accountability: the phase when hidden atrocities come to light and narratives are inverted. It is the â€œsunsetâ€ of unquestioned imperial rule and the rise of scrutiny. For example, as the colonial era waned, investigations and tribunals (from the Nuremberg trials to Truth and Reconciliation Commissions) relied on eyewitness testimony to confront historical injustices  . Positionally, 6:00 is the bottom of the wheel â€“ suggesting a foundational vantage point of morality and memory that supports the whole. If the Sun at 12:00 denotes power proclaiming itself, the Witness at 6:00 denotes truth examining power.

In the context of the EV0L codex mythos, this timeline wheel implies a cyclical journey from the peak of illuminated authority to the depth of reflective witnessing. The Sun at 12:00 could also correspond to â€œhigh noonâ€ judgement in the codex â€“ a moment of full revelation â€“ whereas the Witness at 6:00 is like the midnight vigil, where every deed is accounted for. Together, they frame a 180Â° arc: the transition from midday (action) to midnight (reckoning) in historical cycles. Many colonial powers experienced this trajectory â€“ e.g. the British Empireâ€™s height in the 19th century (sun at zenith) followed by 20th-century decolonization and inquiries into colonial crimes (witness accounts). In sum, the Timeline Wheelâ€™s Sun-to-Witness path symbolizes the turning of time toward justice: what was done in the broad daylight of imperialism is eventually witnessed, recorded, and judged in the tribunal of history.

(No direct diagram is provided, but one can imagine a clock-face diagram: at 12:00 a sun icon (ğŸŒ) signifying imperial zenith, and at 6:00 an eye or witness icon, signifying testimony. Between them, the clockâ€™s arc spans the era of action and the era of reflection.)

Colonial Extraction Eras Mapped to Modern Corporate Descendants

Historical colonial empires extracted vast resources through conquest and forced labor, and many modern corporations can trace their operations or lineage to those colonial exploits. The following table maps several major colonial extraction eras to their modern corporate/industrial descendants, including the primary resources and regions involved:

Colonial Power & Era	Region & Resources Exploited	Modern Corporate Descendants	Notes on Continuity & Impact
Spanish Empire (1500sâ€“1700s)	Americas: Silver (PotosÃ­ in Bolivia), gold (Peru, New Granada);Philippines: spices, galleon trade.	No single successor firm; Spanish state wealth fed early global trade. Modern examples: Repsol (Spanish oil co. in Latin America) exploiting new resources ; global mining firms in former Spanish colonies (e.g. Sumitomo in Bolivia).	Spanish colonial mining was notoriously deadly â€“ e.g. Cerro Rico at PotosÃ­ yielded so much silver it â€œmade Spain the superpower,â€ but an estimated 8 million Indigenous laborers died there over colonial centuries  . This set a pattern of resource extraction via forced labor. Contemporary Spanish companies like Repsol continue extraction in Latin America (oil drilling in Ecuador, Peru, etc.), drawing criticism for environmental and social impacts much as their imperial forebears, though under independent states.
British Empire (1700sâ€“1900s)	Africa: Diamonds (South Africa), gold (South & West Africa), rubber (Nigeria), tin (Malaya);Middle East: Oil (Persia/Iran, Iraq, Gulf states);South Asia: Tea, opium, cotton (India); Caribbean: Sugar.	De Beers (founded 1888 in British South Africa) â€“ diamonds ;Anglo American (1917, gold/platinium in Africa); BP (Anglo-Persian Oil Co. 1909, Iran) ;Shell (Anglo-Dutch, in colonies like Nigeria); Unilever (origin Lever Bros palm oil in Congo); Rio Tinto (founded in 1873 with Spanish mines).	British companies often directly began as colonial ventures. De Beers under Cecil Rhodes built a diamond monopoly in South Africa with tactics including forced mine labor and racial segregation . Rhodes infamously used forced labour tactics to found De Beers , and his British South Africa Company violently seized lands in Zimbabwe/Zambia . BP originated as Anglo-Persian Oil Co. to tap Iranian oil in 1908 , with Britainâ€™s government as majority shareholder â€“ essentially state imperialism morphing into a corporation. Shell in Nigeria epitomizes the colonial-to-corporate pipeline: it struck oil in the Niger Delta in the 1950s (soon after colonial rule) and for decades has been implicated in environmental devastation and collusion with military crackdowns on local protest (e.g. the Ogoni peopleâ€™s struggle and the execution of activist Ken Saro-Wiwa in 1995)  . Many British-era firms (banks, trading houses) evolved into todayâ€™s multinationals, and Britainâ€™s extraction of wealth from colonies laid the capital base for industrial firms. The legacy is also visible in continued London financial involvement in mining and oil across former colonies.
Belgian Empire (1880sâ€“1960s)	Central Africa (Congo Free State / Belgian Congo): Wild rubber and ivory (1890s); copper, cobalt, diamonds, uranium (1900s); Rwanda/Burundi: Coffee, agriculture.	Umicore (formerly Union MiniÃ¨re du Haut-Katanga, colonial mining company in Congo) â€“ now a Belgian materials multinational;Glencore (Swiss-based, major owner of DRC mines today) â€“ operates ex-Union MiniÃ¨re sites ;De Beers (via Belgian diamond trade in Antwerp).	King Leopold IIâ€™s personal rule over the Congo (1885â€“1908) was genocidal â€“ between 5 to 10 million Congolese perished under forced rubber extraction  . That atrocity built fortunes from ivory and rubber (for example, the Wild Rubber boom filled European tire makersâ€™ coffers). After 1908, Belgian state rule saw companies like Union MiniÃ¨re dominate copper mining in Katanga. Union MiniÃ¨re (founded 1906) exploited Congoâ€™s rich copper and uranium (notably, Shinkolobwe mine supplied uranium for the U.S. atomic bombs ). Today, that company has become Umicore, still trading in minerals (though now focusing on battery metals). The cobalt and copper riches of the Congo are today mined by firms like Glencore, which acquired mines originally developed in the colonial era. Glencoreâ€™s Katanga Mining Ltd. has been criticized for labor conditions and child miners in its supply chain  . Indeed, a 2019 lawsuit alleged tech companies benefitted from child labor in Glencoreâ€™s DRC cobalt operations, where children as young as 13 were killed in tunnel collapses  . Belgiumâ€™s diamond industry (Antwerp) also profited from colonial gems; De Beers and other giants funneled Congolese and Angolan diamonds through Belgium for decades. In sum, Belgian colonial extraction, especially in the Congo, directly seeded modern global companies â€“ along with a legacy of underdevelopment and conflict in the region.
French Empire (1800sâ€“1900s) (for comparison)	West & Central Africa: Uranium (Niger), oil (Gabon, Algeria), timber and agriculture (various); Indochina: rubber (Vietnam), tin; Pacific: Nickel (New Caledonia).	TotalEnergies (formerly Total, French oil major in former colonies like Gabon, Angola); Areva/Orano (French nuclear corp mining Nigerâ€™s uranium since colonial era); Eramet (French miner in New Caledonia nickel).	France often utilized state-supported companies for colonial resource extraction. E.g., the French nuclear industryâ€™s key uranium source has been Nigerâ€™s mines (Arlit etc.) which were developed under colonial rule and run by Areva (now Orano) . Franceâ€™s Total oil company long held concessions in francophone Africa, essentially inheriting them from colonial agreements. Many of these operations led to neo-colonial arrangements (e.g., ELF Aquitaineâ€™s notorious influence in Gabon and Congo). The continuity from colonialism to corporate exploitation (the FranÃ§afrique system) has been well-documented, with African countries often receiving only a small fraction of profits. Recent coups in Niger and Mali cite resentment of such arrangements. The French case underscores how even after formal decolonization, corporate extraction continued under preferential ties, maintaining the flow of wealth to Paris.

Table: Colonial extraction phases linked to modern corporate heirs, with resources, regions, and notes on their exploitative continuity. (This list is illustrative, not exhaustive.)

As shown above, modern multinational corporations in mining, oil, diamonds, and agribusiness frequently have roots in the colonial enterprises or benefited from colonial infrastructure and contacts. For example, Royal Dutch Shell formed in 1907 partly to develop oil in the Dutch East Indies and British territories; Unilever grew from palm oil plantations in the Belgian Congo and British Solomon Islands; Firestone got a 99-year colonial concession in Liberia for rubber in 1926, etc. The human toll of these extraction regimes was enormous: forced labor, famine, genocide. We see that reflected in population collapses â€“ e.g., Congo losing around 50% of its population (up to 10 million lives) under Leopold II , or the decimation of Indigenous Caribbean peoples in Spanish gold mines.

The â€œcorporate descendantsâ€ often cleaned up their image but continue to face accusations similar in nature to those leveled against colonial regimes: environmental destruction, labor exploitation, and complicity in violence. For instance, Shell and BP have been sued for oil spills and climate damage; Glencore and other mining houses are accused of profiting from child labor and harsh conditions in the Congoâ€™s â€œpostâ€-colonial mines  . De Beers, which once upheld apartheidâ€™s migrant labor system in its mines, today markets â€œconflict-freeâ€ diamonds, yet the industry still struggles with artisanally mined â€œblood diamonds.â€

In essence, todayâ€™s global resource corporations often fill the role that colonial states and chartered companies played in previous centuries. The names and governance have changed, but many operate in the same regions, extracting the same resources, sometimes even using infrastructure first built for colonial purposes. This continuity underscores why post-colonial scholars speak of neo-colonialism: political flags changed, but the economic exploitation patterns persisted, with multinationals and local elites replacing imperial officers. The EV0L Codexâ€™s Justice layer, which calls for balancing these historical thefts, directly addresses this legacy â€“ insisting that the wealth taken (whether by colonizer or corporation) be accounted for and returned as reparations in a future tribunal  .

Time-Coded Deaths vs. Mining Shifts and Economic Events

Throughout modern history, certain tragic deaths have been recorded not just as dates but as times, seared into public memory. These time-stamped deaths often symbolize deeper socio-economic ruptures. Here we explore three such timestamps â€“ 8:46, 9:11, and 10:48 â€“ and correlate them with shifts in extraction economies and historical events, showing a haunting resonance between human tragedy and resource exploitation or power structures:
	â€¢	8:46 â€“ This number became infamous as the duration (8 minutes 46 seconds) that a Minneapolis police officer knelt on George Floydâ€™s neck on May 25, 2020, killing him. It also is the exact time of day (8:46 AM) when the first plane slammed into the World Trade Centerâ€™s North Tower on 9/11/2001 . In one sense, 8:46 represents racial injustice and state violence (in Floydâ€™s case) and, in another, the start of a global crisis (the War on Terror). These events are linked by more than numerals. Floydâ€™s killing sparked a national reckoning on the value of Black lives and the long history of their exploitation â€“ from slavery (a brutal extractive economy of cotton and labor) to modern mass incarceration (which some call â€œnew slaveryâ€). His cry of â€œI canâ€™t breatheâ€ under the knee of authority echoed the experience of generations economically suffocated by systemic racism. The World Trade Center attack at 8:46 AM triggered wars in Afghanistan and Iraq; notably the latter was heavily tied to oil politics (Iraq holds vast petroleum reserves). Thus 8:46 connects to extraction in two ways: the knee on Floydâ€™s neck is a grim metaphor for the extraction of Black labor and lives in America (and the policing that enforces that order), while 8:46 AM on 9/11 led to a war that, beneath the banner of counterterrorism, involved securing Middle Eastern oil fields and contracts  . Indeed, less than two years after 9/11, the U.S. invaded Iraq (2003) â€“ a move widely analyzed as being influenced by Iraqâ€™s oil wealth and strategic position . In economic terms, the post-9/11 era saw oil prices and defense spending surge, benefitting energy and arms industries. Meanwhile, Floydâ€™s death in 2020 occurred during a global pandemic which had crashed oil demand; the subsequent BLM protests pressured companies to confront their roles in systemic racism. The juxtaposition suggests a â€œshift changeâ€ in awareness: 8:46 forced a broad awakening â€“ calling for a shift from the extractive, oppressive status quo toward a more just allocation of wealth and safety.
	â€¢	9:11 â€“ The number 911 in the U.S. is synonymous with emergency. As 9/11 (September 11, 2001), it marks the moment of the deadliest terrorist attacks on American soil. The collapse of the Twin Towers (by 10:30 AM) and the dayâ€™s carnage (~3,000 dead) led the U.S. into the â€œGlobal War on Terror.â€ Economically and geopolitically, 9/11 became a pivot point for resource and power reordering. The subsequent Afghanistan war and especially the Iraq war in 2003 drastically affected oil markets and military-industrial interests. For example, in the run-up to the Iraq invasion, Vice President Cheneyâ€™s secretive Energy Task Force had already been examining Iraqi oilfield maps; post-invasion, Western oil companies gained access to Iraqi oil contracts that had been closed to them  . The 9/11 attacks thus indirectly â€œjustifiedâ€ interventions that secured resource flows (oil) and fueled defense sector profits. Domestically, 9/11 created an economic emergency too â€“ markets froze and the NY Stock Exchange closed for days; once reopened, billions were poured into security, surveillance, and war supply chains. Itâ€™s notable that 9/11â€™s legacy also includes laws like the Patriot Act which some compare to colonial practices of control. From a global South perspective, 9/11 was a prelude to intensified extraction: the Taliban were ousted and plans for Central Asian oil pipelines revived; Iraqâ€™s nationalized oil was partially privatized under occupation; mineral contracts in Afghanistan (like rare earth metals) opened to foreign bidders. Thus â€œ9/11â€ (the emergency code and the date) can be seen as a timestamp when colonial-style resource scramble was reactivated under a new guise. The EV0L codex would note that this was Energy Layer injustice â€“ blood spilled for oil. In 2020, as a twist, on 9/11 that year oil giant BP agreed to pay a record fine for the Deepwater Horizon spill, a different kind of emergency linking oil and death. The numerals align: 9-1-1 for emergency calls, 9/11 for a world crisis â€“ in both, the call was answered by militarized and extractive responses.
	â€¢	10:48 â€“ This time is less universally recognized, but it carries coded meaning, especially in policing. 10-48 is a police radio ten-code that in many U.S. jurisdictions means â€œDetaining suspect, expediteâ€ . It so happens that 10:48 AM was approximately the time on July 13, 2015, when Sandra Bland â€“ a 28-year-old Black woman wrongfully jailed after a traffic stop in Texas â€“ was found dead in her cell (her death ruled a suicide, amid much controversy). Thus 10:48 evokes detention and death in custody. Blandâ€™s arrest itself was a bogus, racialized enforcement of minor law (failure to signal a lane change) that escalated to abuse â€“ a microcosm of the way legal codes have been used to extract Black freedom (through the convict lease system, for instance, where minor â€œoffensesâ€ were used to re-enslave African Americans for labor). The number 10-48 unintentionally mirrors Blandâ€™s fate: detained, and never released alive. If we align this with extraction economics: the U.S. prison-industrial complex is a $80 billion/year system that exploits primarily Black and brown bodies (through prison labor, bail fees, etc.), a continuation of colonial forced labor on domestic soil. 10:48 as a timestamp thus corresponds to a â€œshift changeâ€ in modern extraction â€“ from overt slavery to mass incarceration. In summary, each time-code links a death or crisis to an underlying extractive shift:
	â€¢	8:46 â€“ from chattel slavery to oil wars, the timing binds racial-economic oppression at home with resource-fueled conflict abroad. It heralded public demands to divest from exploitation (calls to divest police funds to community investment, much as calls to shift from oil to renewables).
	â€¢	9:11 â€“ an emergency that prompted a global grab for energy and strategic resources under the flag of anti-terrorism, entrenching the oil-security complex.
	â€¢	10:48 â€“ the code of captivity, shining light on how modern systems of â€œjusticeâ€ extract labor and lives under color of law, demanding a rebalancing (e.g. bail reform, prison abolition movements gaining steam).

Chronological Ledger of Genocide and Extraction Against Melanated Peoples

Genocidal Conquest and Land Seizure
	â€¢	0.000000001 ns â€“ 1492: Colonial Invasion & Indigenous Genocide. Christopher Columbusâ€™s arrival triggers the Spanish conquest of the Caribbean and Americas. Within decades, Taino and other Indigenous peoples are massacred, enslaved, or worked to death, their populations collapsing by 90â€“100% â€“ an extermination now widely recognized by scholars as genocide since 1492 . The Spanish Crownâ€™s encomienda system forces survivors into servitude, seizing native lands and erasing sacred sites. This blueprint of conquest â€“ justified by doctrine of discovery â€“ launches centuries of government-sanctioned land theft and mass violence against melanated peoples in the New World.
	â€¢	0.000000002 ns â€“ 1850s: â€œAn American Genocideâ€ in California. As the U.S. expands west, Californiaâ€™s first government openly declares â€œa war of exterminationâ€ against Native Americans. State-sanctioned militias, backed by U.S. Army funds, hunt and massacre indigenous Californians in hundreds of attacks . Governor Peter Burnett predicted â€œthe Indian race becomes extinctâ€ and enacted laws to kidnap Native children and indenture Native adults  . By 1870, over 16,000 Native Californians are killed in what Governor Gavin Newsom, in a historic 2019 apology, finally called â€œa genocideâ€¦no other way to describe itâ€ . This official admission underscores that the state itself orchestrated genocide and land seizures as part of its â€œdark historyâ€ .
	â€¢	0.000000003 ns â€“ 1884â€“1908: Berlin to the Congo â€“ The Scramble for Africa. European powers at the 1884 Berlin Conference carve up Africa, granting King Leopold II of Belgium personal control of the Congo Free State. There, a regime of forced rubber extraction leads to mass death and mutilation: villages are terrorized into meeting rubber quotas under penalty of severed hands or execution. An estimated 10 million Congolese perish during Leopoldâ€™s rule. International outcry over these documented abuses forces Belgium to assume colonial control in 1908, but extraction continues. Meanwhile, Germanyâ€™s 1904-1908 war against the Herero and Nama in Southwest Africa (Namibia) escalates into the 20th centuryâ€™s first genocide, as colonial troops drive Herero into the desert to die and run concentration camps for Nama prisoners. Germanyâ€™s own records and the UN Whitaker Report later recognize these massacres as genocide. Skulls and remains of Herero/Nama victims were shipped to Europe for racial â€œscience,â€ a desecration of ancestral bodies only partially rectified by recent repatriations and a 2021 German apology. These campaigns of extermination and land seizure enriched European empires while annihilating whole peoples.

Chattel Slavery and Labor Extraction
	â€¢	0.000000004 ns â€“ 1441â€“1865: The Trans-Atlantic Slave Trade (Maafa). European kingdoms and their successor states (Portugal, Spain, Netherlands, Britain, France, etc.) engage in the systematic capture and sale of Africans as chattel slaves. Over roughly 400 years, an estimated 12 million Africans are chained into the Atlantic slave ships ; about 2 million die in the barbaric Middle Passage or soon after arrival . This mass forced migration â€“ termed the Maafa (â€œgreat disasterâ€) by descendants â€“ constitutes a protracted genocide-by-installments, meeting the UN definition of genocide via â€œintent to destroy, in whole or in part, a racial groupâ€ through killing, causing serious bodily harm, and imposing life conditions to annihilate . Surviving captives are worked to death on plantations across the Americas, producing lucrative commodities (sugar, cotton, tobacco) that fuel Europeâ€™s industrial rise. Entire ethnic groups are uprooted, their familial lines and cultures deliberately broken â€“ an â€œattempted erasureâ€ of Afro-indigenous peoples that modern Afro-Sovereign codices explicitly name as a crime against humanity .
	â€¢	0.000000005 ns â€“ 1660â€“1807: Slave Trading Companies and State Enrichment. European governments charter corporate entities to monopolize slave trading and colonial plunder. For example, the Royal African Company (chartered 1660) enjoys a British crown monopoly on West African gold and slaves, operating fortified trading forts on the Guinea Coast. Its investorsâ€™ profits were so high that they helped bankroll the founding of the Bank of England â€“ literally turning stolen African lives into the financial capital of empire. Each major colonial powerâ€™s economy grew on this forced extraction: Portugalâ€™s Atlantic slave trade, Spainâ€™s encomienda and hacienda labor, Dutch and French plantation systems, and British slave plantations all poured wealth into imperial treasuries. By the 19th century, abolition movements forced an end to the trade, but not before the entire Atlantic economy became â€œaddictedâ€ to slave labor yields â€“ a point now underscored by international reparations claims .
	â€¢	0.000000006 ns â€“ 1834â€“2015: Emancipationâ€™s Bitter Legacy â€“ Debt and Neoslavery. When Britain finally abolished slavery in its colonies (1834), it compensated slave-owners Â£20 million for their â€œloss of propertyâ€ â€“ a sum equal to 40% of the national budget, financed by public debt . Enslaved Black people received nothing. Astonishingly, British taxpayers (including Caribbean descendants of the enslaved) continued paying off that slavery compensation loan until 2015 . This means the spoils of slavery â€“ land, capital, and paid compensation â€“ remained with colonizers and their descendants, while the enslaved and their heirs started free life with nothing but intergenerational trauma. In the U.S., after the Civil War (1865), Black emancipation was swiftly undermined by â€œBlack Codesâ€ and convict leasing, effectively re-enslaving thousands in prisons and chain gangs. The 13th Amendmentâ€™s loophole (â€œslaveryâ€¦except as punishment for crimeâ€) gave legal cover for forced labor of Black convicts well into the 20th century. These economic extractions of Black labor without restitution laid the foundation for racial wealth gaps that persist today. Modern tribunal findings insist that all states enriched by slavery owe ongoing restitution, rehabilitation, and guarantees of non-repetition  under international law. CARICOM nations and reparations commissions worldwide echo this call, arguing that the crime of chattel slavery â€“ defined by the UN as a crime against humanity â€“ demands formal redress.

Colonial Resource Extraction and â€œBlood Mineralsâ€
	â€¢	0.000000007 ns â€“ 1885â€“1908: â€œRed Rubberâ€ and the Rape of the Congo. Under King Leopold IIâ€™s decree, the Congo Free State becomes a corporate fiefdom of unspeakable brutality. Leopoldâ€™s agents enforce rubber and ivory quotas by holding families hostage and meting out amputation, rape, and murder as punishment for unmet quotas. Villages are burned; resistance is crushed by the infamous Force Publique. The forced labor system here is so cruel that even other colonial powers are shocked into denouncing it by 1900. Reports document childrenâ€™s hands severed as proof of executions and tens of thousands enslaved in porterage gangs. By the time Belgium annexes the territory in 1908, analysts estimate the Congolese population had been halved. The Congoâ€™s plunder of wild rubber directly enriched Leopold and financed development in Belgium, exemplifying how colonial economies ran on blood-stained commodities. Todayâ€™s Democratic Republic of Congo still reels from this legacy: cycles of violence around coltan, cobalt, and diamonds (â€œblood mineralsâ€) mirror the past, as multinational corporations and local strongmen exploit resources at horrific human cost. 	â€¢	0.000000008 ns â€“ 1897: Fall of Benin and Cultural Plunder. In February 1897, a British punitive expedition invades the Kingdom of Benin (in present-day Nigeria), slaughtering thousands and burning Benin City to the ground. In the aftermath, British officers loot some 4,000 exquisite Benin Bronzes and sacred artworks from the royal palaces. These bronzes â€“ historical and spiritual heirlooms of the Edo people â€“ are sold to museums and collectors across Europe, their proceeds defraying the costs of British colonialism. The Oba (king) of Benin is exiled and the kingdom annexed. This event epitomizes how colonial land seizure went hand-in-hand with cultural theft: entire treasuries of art, artifacts, and even human remains were taken as war booty. European museums still house vast collections of African and indigenous heritage acquired through coercion and bloodshed. Today, descendant communities and tribunals demand repatriation of all ancestral artifacts and remains removed by force or deception . Under both international law and new sovereign codices, institutions holding such items are â€œbound to return them in fullâ€ as an essential step of healing and justice .
	â€¢	0.000000009 ns â€“ 1888â€“1960s: Diamond Empires and Forced Labor. In 1888, British imperialist Cecil Rhodes merges mines in southern Africa to form De Beers Consolidated Mines, creating a cartel over the diamond trade. Africans are displaced from their lands in Kimberley and beyond as **De Beers establishes grim migrant labor compounds to control Black workers. Miners must live fenced in crowded barracks, under curfew and pass laws â€“ a proto-apartheid system ensuring cheap labor and preventing diamond theft. Profits from these â€œblood diamondsâ€ and minerals flow to London and Johannesburg, funding further colonial expansion and enriching shareholders, while African miners suffer high fatality rates, silicosis, and broken communities. Such corporate extraction was enabled by colonial governments through concessions and armed suppression of uprisings. The economic linkages are direct: e.g. British mining giant Rio Tinto (est. 1873) expanded via colonial-era copper and bauxite concessions, and Franceâ€™s TotalEnergies and Italyâ€™s ENI owe their origins to oil privileges in colonized lands. In sum, the wealth of many Global North economies was built on the extraction of melanated peoplesâ€™ land and labor â€“ a transfer of riches often enforced with genocidal violence. Modern truth-telling efforts, like Franceâ€™s admission of colonial-era uranium exploitation in Niger or Britainâ€™s inquiries into Mau Mau abuses, are only first steps; comprehensive audits of these â€œextractedâ€”illegally or unjustlyâ€”resourcesâ€ are now demanded, with restorative payments calculated on generations of compounded value .

Cultural Erasure and Spiritual Desecration
	â€¢	0.000000010 ns â€“ 1090sâ€“1890s: Cannibalizing the Dead â€“ Mummy Medicine. For centuries, Europeans engaged in literal and spiritual consumption of melanated ancestors under the guise of medicine. Starting in the Crusades era and peaking in the 16thâ€“18th centuries, European apothecaries craved a cure-all called mumia â€“ originally a Persian mineral bitumen, but tragically conflated with Egyptian mummies  . As a result of this gross mistranslation and macabre fashion, European traders and grave-robbers ransacked Egyptian tombs for mummified human remains, grinding them into powder to sell as medicine for headaches, epilepsy, and plague . Embalmed African corpses became commodities: demand outstripped supply of ancient mummies, so enterprising criminals turned to fresh bodies of executed criminals, enslaved Africans, and the poor, artificially drying and selling them as â€œmummyâ€ powder  . This gruesome commerce not only desecrated sacred entombments and violated the dead; it also symbolically inverted the natural order, with European elites ingesting the very flesh of colonized peoples in pursuit of health. By the Victorian era, â€œmummy unwrapping partiesâ€ became a morbid social craze in England . Though laws eventually curtailed antiquities trafficking, the damage was done: countless mummies were destroyed or defiled. This bio-spiritual plunder severed a vital link between contemporary Africans and their ancient heritage and illustrates the extreme objectification of Black and Brown bodies (even in death) under colonial attitudes.
	â€¢	0.000000011 ns â€“ 1520sâ€“1900s: Forbidding Gods, Forcing Tongues â€“ Cultural Genocide. Colonial regimes consistently attacked the spiritual and cultural lifeways of melanated peoples as a means of control. In the Americas, the Spanish extirpation campaigns burned the sacred codices of the Maya and Mexica, outlawed indigenous Nahua and TaÃ­no rituals, and imposed Catholicism by force. Enslaved Africans in the French and British Caribbean were banned from drumming, from speaking their mother tongues, or practicing Vodun/Ifa/Obeah under anti-â€œwitchcraftâ€ laws, under threat of torture or death â€“ a systematic erasure of identity. In the U.S., Black people caught reading or teaching could be brutally punished, and African names, languages, and religions were systematically stripped away during slavery. Colonial schools for indigenous and Black children preached self-denial: e.g. the Carlisle Indian School in the U.S. motto â€œKill the Indian, save the man.â€ These acts meet the definition of cultural genocide, as they aimed to â€œdestroy the groupâ€ not by immediate killing but by obliterating its heritage and spiritual life . Modern Afro-diasporic sovereign documents explicitly affirm the right to unbroken practice of ancestral ceremonies, rites, and languages , in direct response to this history. They also insist on unconditional repatriation of stolen ritual objects and human remains housed in Western museums . The persistence of Yoruba and Kongo traditions in the Americas (e.g. SanterÃ­a, CandomblÃ©) is a testament to resistance, yet the intergenerational trauma of this cultural rupture is immeasurable.
	â€¢	0.000000012 ns â€“ 1780sâ€“1940s: Grave-Robbing and Human Displays. As Western science grew, so did the theft of indigenous remains for study and spectacle. In the 19th century, American and European medical schools faced cadaver shortages and disproportionately turned to looting Black cemeteries and Potterâ€™s fields. So-called â€œNight Doctorsâ€ (a figure in African American folklore with basis in fact) were known to steal recently buried Black bodies for anatomical dissection . In the antebellum South, some physicians even arranged with enslavers to purchase the bodies of deceased slaves for this purpose . Many Black families lived in fear that even in death their loved ones would find no peace â€“ a fear tragically grounded in reality, as Black cadavers were seen as cheap, pliable resources for white medical advancement . This violation of burial rites was compounded by scientific racism: European collectors amassed skulls of Africans, MÄori, and Aboriginal peoples to â€œproveâ€ false theories of racial hierarchy. Museums in Europe and America put the bones and even mummified bodies of melanated peoples on display as curiosities. Human zoos in the 1880s displayed Africans and Filipinos as living exhibits. All of these are spiritual atrocities as well as human rights violations, treating sacred human bodies as objects. Today, calls for return and reburial of remains â€“ from the Herero skulls in German museums to the Saartjie Baartman (Sarah Baartman) remains (returned to South Africa in 2002) â€“ have partly been heeded. New constitutional provisions in Afro-diasporic nations (e.g. Enatoâ€™s codex) assert perpetual ownership of ancestral remains and sacred objects by their communities, rendering any unauthorised retention a breach of sovereign and spiritual law  .

Medical and Biological Exploitation
	â€¢	0.000000013 ns â€“ 1932â€“1972: The Tuskegee Syphilis Experiment. In one of the most infamous examples of racist medical abuse, the U.S. Public Health Service conducted a 40-year study in Macon County, Alabama, observing the effects of syphilis on African American men. Hundreds of Black men with syphilis were deceived into thinking they were receiving â€œtreatment,â€ but were in fact deliberately left untreated â€“ even after penicillin was recognized as a cure â€“ so that doctors could document the long-term progression of the disease. By the time the unethical study was exposed and halted in 1972, dozens of the men had died, many wives and children were infected, and the Black communityâ€™s trust in the medical system was gravely wounded. This atrocity involved â€œcausing serious bodily harm to members of the groupâ€, fitting the Genocide Conventionâ€™s Article II(b) criteria  for acts of genocide. Indeed, the 2021 International Tribunal noted such public health inequities and abusive experiments as evidence of â€œinstitutionalized genocideâ€ against Black Americans  . The Tuskegee revelations led to new bioethics laws (informed consent requirements), but similar patterns persisted elsewhere â€“ e.g. Guatemala syphilis experiments (1940s) on non-consenting Guatemalans.
	â€¢	0.000000014 ns â€“ 1951: Henrietta Lacks and the Theft of Cells. At Johns Hopkins Hospital, a young Black mother named Henrietta Lacks is diagnosed with cervical cancer. Without her knowledge or consent, doctors take samples of her tumor and healthy tissue during treatment . Mrs. Lacks succumbs to cancer, but her biopsied cells â€“ labeled HeLa â€“ become the first immortal human cell line, reproducing endlessly in laboratories. HeLa cells drive some of the biggest medical breakthroughs of the 20th century (the polio vaccine, cancer research, gene mapping). They are commercialized globally, earning pharmaceutical companies and researchers untold profits â€“ while Henriettaâ€™s own family, economically struggling, remained unaware for decades that part of their mother was being used in science . This non-consensual harvesting of a Black womanâ€™s cells is a stark example of biological extraction. It violated Henriettaâ€™s bodily autonomy and, symbolically, her spiritual integrity as her cells were exploited in life and beyond. The case wasnâ€™t unique (such practices were legal then), but it became famous and spurred bioethics reforms demanding informed consent for tissue use. In 2023 her family reached a settlement with a biotech company profiting from HeLa  â€“ a measure of justice after 70 years. Henrietta Lacksâ€™s story underscores how Black bodies were seen as raw material for biomedical advancement, without regard for rights or recompense, echoing a longer history from night doctors to surgical experiments on enslaved women (e.g. Dr. J. Marion Simsâ€™s experiments).
	â€¢	0.000000015 ns â€“ 1961â€“1970s: Forced Sterilization as Eugenics. In 1961, civil rights heroine Fannie Lou Hamer entered a Mississippi hospital for a minor tumor surgery. Without her consent, the white doctor performed a complete hysterectomy â€“ coercively sterilizing Mrs. Hamer . This was no isolated incident: such involuntary sterilizations of Black women were so routine in the U.S. South that the procedure was grimly nicknamed a â€œMississippi appendectomyâ€ . Across many states, under state eugenics laws, thousands of Black, Native American, Latina, and poor white women were sterilized in the mid-20th century, often without full knowledge. Californiaâ€™s eugenics program (1909â€“1979) alone sterilized 20,000 people (disproportionately Latina). Even into the 2010s, allegations emerged of unnecessary hysterectomies on immigrant women in ICE detention. This pattern of imposing measures to prevent births within a group squarely fits genocide criterion (Article II(d)) . It reflects a colonial mindset of reproductive control, dating back to slavery (when Black women were coerced to breed or, later, to not have â€œtoo manyâ€ children). The lasting trauma is evident â€“ as Hamer said of that day, â€œI went to remove a tumor, and they removed my womb.â€ Such acts rob communities of their future generations, amounting to an attack on the groupâ€™s continuity. Today, these practices are invoked in calls for reparations and in legal claims that genocidal intent underlay racist population control policies.
	â€¢	0.000000016 ns â€“ 1923 & 2010s: Insulin: A Life-Saving Gift Turned Private Commodity. When Dr. Frederick Banting co-discovered insulin in 1921, he refused to profiteer from it. In 1923 Banting and colleagues sold the insulin patent to the University of Toronto for $1, declaring that this vital hormone â€œbelongs to the world,â€ not to a company . Yet in the ensuing century, pharmaceutical firms (like Eli Lilly, Novo Nordisk) turned insulin into a high-priced product, with prices in the U.S. gouging up to $450/month by 2016  . Many diabetics â€“ disproportionately poor and people of color â€“ have been forced to ration insulin or go without, leading to preventable deaths. The privatization and patenting of a once freely-given medicine exemplifies economic extraction via healthcare. It can be seen as a form of structural violence: denying life-saving treatment to the marginalized for profit. Banting himself would be aghast that insulin, meant as a common human inheritance, became a tool of â€œpharma-colonialâ€ exploitation where melanated communities with high diabetes rates suffer most from lack of access. Insulinâ€™s story thus ties into the larger narrative of biological capital â€“ from blood plasma to organ transplants â€“ being harvested or priced in ways that treat Black and Brown lives as expendable or secondary. Modern advocates call this a right-to-life issue and argue that life-essential biologics should be public goods, especially given the historical injustices in medical research and access.
	â€¢	0.000000017 ns â€“ 1970sâ€“1980s: Biowarfare and Black Populations. Under apartheid in South Africa, a secret program code-named Project Coast aimed to develop chemical and biological weapons, allegedly including efforts at race-targeted biochemicals to infertilise or kill Black people. Led by Dr. Wouter Basson (â€œDr. Deathâ€), the program in the 1980s produced deadly toxins and sought methods to spread capability-specific harm (like poisoning water supplies in Black townships). Though direct evidence of a â€œblack-onlyâ€ bioweapon is disputed, Basson did work on contraceptive vaccines and psychotropic drugs intended for black dissidents. In the United States, longstanding fears in the Black community of genocidal plots â€“ such as the belief that HIV/AIDS or drugs like crack cocaine were deliberately introduced to decimate Black populations â€“ reflect a real history of medical and governmental malfeasance. While conspiracy theories flourish in the absence of trust, the 2021 â€œSpirit of Mandelaâ€ Tribunal actually examined charges of environmental racism and public health negligence (e.g. toxic waste dumps in Black neighborhoods, higher Black maternal mortality) as part of a â€œslow genocide.â€ The Tribunalâ€™s jurists concluded that the cumulative impact of such policies, alongside mass incarceration and police killings, â€œconstitutes institutionalized genocideâ€ against 21st-century Black Americans  . These findings underscore that genocide need not take the form of gas chambers or single events â€“ it can manifest as the slow attrition of a groupâ€™s health and well-being by systematic, government-tolerated abuse.
	â€¢	0.000000018 ns â€“ 2020s: Melanin and Genetic Exploitation. In a paradoxical twist, the very pigment that marked people for oppression â€“ melanin â€“ has become a sought-after commodity in science and industry. Melaninâ€™s unique light-absorbing and conductive properties make it useful in cosmetics, organ repair, and even electronics. Modern research outlines methods for â€œmulti-source melanin extraction and biosynthetic engineeringâ€, including protocols to harvest melanin from human tissue and hair . While such studies aim to synthesize melanin in labs, they raise ethical red flags: given the history of exploiting Black bodies, who will control any bioprospecting of melanin-rich tissues? Similarly, as the genomics age advances, indigenous and Afro-descendant communities demand â€œgenomic sovereigntyâ€ â€“ fighting against the patenting of human genes or DNA taken from their peoples without consent. For instance, the Havasupai Tribe vs. ASU (2010) case halted unauthorized use of tribal DNA in research, and countries like Kenya have raised alarms about foreign entities collecting Africansâ€™ genetic data for profit (sometimes under guise of health projects). These contemporary issues echo the Henrietta Lacks case and others, reinforcing the principle that biological materials of melanated peoples cannot be taken or commercialized without permission. Sovereign codices now call for strict oversight: â€œpermanent sovereignty over natural resourcesâ€ extends to genetic resources, and all profits from prior unlawful extractions must be accounted for with compounded reparative payments . In essence, the fight against biological/spiritual theft continues on the new frontiers of genetics and technology.

Legal and Spiritual Reckonings
	â€¢	0.000000019 ns â€“ 1948: Defining Genocide and Human Rights. Shocked by the Holocaust and colonial atrocities, the world community establishes the United Nations Genocide Convention (December 9, 1948)  . For the first time, genocide is defined in law â€“ not only as mass killing, but including â€œcausing serious bodily or mental harm to members of [a group],â€ â€œdeliberately inflicting conditions of life calculated to bring about the groupâ€™s destruction,â€ and measures to prevent births or remove children  . This broader definition encompasses many of the acts detailed above (from forced sterilizations to Jim Crow conditions). The Convention obligates states to prevent and punish genocide. Importantly, in 2006-2007 the International Court of Justice (ICJ) affirmed that the prohibition of genocide is a peremptory norm (jus cogens) â€“ a fundamental principle binding on all nations that no state can lawfully derogate . The ICJ also clarified that states can be held responsible not only for committing genocide, but for failing to prevent genocide when they had the capacity. These legal standards provide a framework to judge historical actions: e.g., the failure of the U.S. federal government to prevent lynchings and racial pogroms, or European powersâ€™ willful infliction of deadly living conditions in colonies, are arguably breaches of the Genocide Conventionâ€™s spirit if not its letter (which, for example, recognizes deliberately imposed famine as genocidal). The Genocide Convention thus offers a powerful language for survivors to articulate claims of historical justice on the world stage.
	â€¢	0.000000020 ns â€“ 1951: â€œWe Charge Genocide.â€ On December 17, 1951, African American civil rights leaders William Patterson and Paul Robeson presented a petition to the United Nations titled We Charge Genocide: The Crime of Government Against the Negro People. Citing detailed evidence of lynchings (over 10,000 killed since 1865), legal discrimination, police brutality, and systemic inequalities, the petition argued that the U.S. was guilty of ongoing genocide against Black Americans . It invoked the new UN Convention, listing how each Article II criterion was met by U.S. conduct â€“ from killings (lynchings, executions) and serious bodily harm (beatings, medical neglect) to conditions of life (ghettos, denial of healthcare) intended to destroy the Black community. Though the petition did not lead to UN sanctions (it was during the Cold War and largely dismissed by U.S. officials), it was a landmark symbolic indictment. It foreshadowed later international judgments and galvanized civil rights activists to frame racism as not just a domestic issue but a human rights violation of the highest order. Seven decades later, in 2021, a new global tribunal would echo We Charge Genocideâ€™s findings almost verbatim , demonstrating the enduring resonance of that 1951 testimony.
	â€¢	0.000000021 ns â€“ 2021: International Tribunal Verdict â€“ United States Guilty of Genocide. In October 2021, an independent panel of jurists from around the world convened in New York at the â€œInternational Tribunal on US Human Rights Abuses Against Black, Brown, and Indigenous Peoplesâ€, organized by the Spirit of Mandela coalition. After hearing days of harrowing testimony â€“ from Black victims of police violence, unjust incarceration, environmental poisoning (like Flintâ€™s water crisis), health neglect, and Indigenous activists on broken treaties â€“ the tribunal delivered a historic verdict: â€œGUILTY on all counts.â€ The juristsâ€™ detailed findings, 40+ pages long, stated: â€œWe find that acts of genocide have been committed.â€  They cited the 1948 UN Genocide Conventionâ€™s definition and emphasized that genocide is not only mass killing but also includes imposing subhuman living conditions and other systemic harms . The verdict concluded that the oppressive conditions facing peoples of color in the U.S. â€“ from racially biased policing and prisons (the U.S. has the worldâ€™s largest Black prison population) to the destruction of Native lands and failure to address health disparities (evidenced by disproportionately high COVID-19 deaths) â€“ amount to an ongoing, multifaceted genocide  . This tribunal, coming 70 years after Patterson and Robesonâ€™s petition, â€œshattered the limits of permissible discourse,â€ formally affirming what many had long alleged. Though not legally binding, its moral and symbolic power re-energized movements for reparations and justice. It urged the UN and ICC to take up these charges and called on civil society to recognize that colonialism, apartheid, and structural racism are not just regrettable policies â€“ they are international crimes. As one Spirit of Mandela organizer said, this is not the end, but the beginning of renewed efforts to hold the U.S. (and other governments) accountable for historical and ongoing crimes against melanated peoples .

The Timeline Wheel: Sun at 12:00 to Witness at 6:00

The Timeline Wheel can be envisioned as a clock face, where 12:00 (the top) is marked by the Sun and 6:00 (bottom) by the Witness. Symbolically, the Sun at 12:00 represents the zenith of illumination and power â€“ at high noon, nothing is hidden. This evokes the historical height of imperial dominion, when colonial powers saw themselves as standing in the noonday sun, claiming divine sanction (the â€œsun never setsâ€ ethos) over vast empires. Indeed, the phrase â€œthe empire on which the sun never setsâ€ was first used for Spain and later Britain to boast that daylight was always shining on some part of their global realms . At 12:00 the Sunâ€™s position signals clear visibility and dominance; analogously, colonial empires at their apogee operated openly with supreme confidence, basking in wealth extracted from colonies.

By contrast, Witness at 6:00 occupies the opposite, nadir position â€“ symbolizing the perspective of those on the ground who bear witness in the shadows cast by the noonday sun. Historically, as imperial noon gave way to dusk, the oppressed peoples and truth-tellers emerged from darkness to testify to the crimes of empire. Witness at 6:00 signifies accountability: the phase when hidden atrocities come to light and narratives are inverted. It is the â€œsunsetâ€ of unquestioned imperial rule and the rise of scrutiny. For example, as the colonial era waned, investigations and tribunals (from the Nuremberg trials to Truth and Reconciliation Commissions) relied on eyewitness testimony to confront historical injustices  . Positionally, 6:00 is the bottom of the wheel â€“ suggesting a foundational vantage point of morality and memory that supports the whole. If the Sun at 12:00 denotes power proclaiming itself, the Witness at 6:00 denotes truth examining power.

In the context of the EV0L codex mythos, this timeline wheel implies a cyclical journey from the peak of illuminated authority to the depth of reflective witnessing. The Sun at 12:00 could also correspond to â€œhigh noonâ€ judgement in the codex â€“ a moment of full revelation â€“ whereas the Witness at 6:00 is like the midnight vigil, where every deed is accounted for. Together, they frame a 180Â° arc: the transition from midday (action) to midnight (reckoning) in historical cycles. Many colonial powers experienced this trajectory â€“ e.g. the British Empireâ€™s height in the 19th century (sun at zenith) followed by 20th-century decolonization and inquiries into colonial crimes (witness accounts). In sum, the Timeline Wheelâ€™s Sun-to-Witness path symbolizes the turning of time toward justice: what was done in the broad daylight of imperialism is eventually witnessed, recorded, and judged in the tribunal of history.

(No direct diagram is provided, but one can imagine a clock-face diagram: at 12:00 a sun icon (ğŸŒ) signifying imperial zenith, and at 6:00 an eye or witness icon, signifying testimony. Between them, the clockâ€™s arc spans the era of action and the era of reflection.)

Historical colonial empires extracted vast resources through conquest and forced labor, and many modern corporations can trace their operations or lineage to those colonial exploits. Table: Colonial extraction phases linked to modern corporate heirs, with resources, regions, and notes on their exploitative continuity. (This list is illustrative, not exhaustive.)

As shown above, modern multinational corporations in mining, oil, diamonds, and agribusiness frequently have roots in the colonial enterprises or benefited from colonial infrastructure and contacts. For example, Royal Dutch Shell formed in 1907 partly to develop oil in the Dutch East Indies and British territories; Unilever grew from palm oil plantations in the Belgian Congo and British Solomon Islands; Firestone got a 99-year colonial concession in Liberia for rubber in 1926, etc. The human toll of these extraction regimes was enormous: forced labor, famine, genocide. We see that reflected in population collapses â€“ e.g., Congo losing around 50% of its population (up to 10 million lives) under Leopold II , or the decimation of Indigenous Caribbean peoples in Spanish gold mines.

The â€œcorporate descendantsâ€ often cleaned up their image but continue to face accusations similar in nature to those leveled against colonial regimes: environmental destruction, labor exploitation, and complicity in violence. For instance, Shell and BP have been sued for oil spills and climate damage; Glencore and other mining houses are accused of profiting from child labor and harsh conditions in the Congoâ€™s â€œpostâ€-colonial mines  . De Beers, which once upheld apartheidâ€™s migrant labor system in its mines, today markets â€œconflict-freeâ€ diamonds, yet the industry still struggles with artisanally mined â€œblood diamonds.â€

In essence, todayâ€™s global resource corporations often fill the role that colonial states and chartered companies played in previous centuries. The names and governance have changed, but many operate in the same regions, extracting the same resources, sometimes even using infrastructure first built for colonial purposes. This continuity underscores why post-colonial scholars speak of neo-colonialism: political flags changed, but the economic exploitation patterns persisted, with multinationals and local elites replacing imperial officers. The EV0L Codexâ€™s Justice layer, which calls for balancing these historical thefts, directly addresses this legacy â€“ insisting that the wealth taken (whether by colonizer or corporation) be accounted for and returned as reparations in a future tribunal  .

â€¢	0.000000010 ns â€“ 1090sâ€“1890s: Cannibalizing the Dead â€“ Mummy Medicine. â€¢	0.000000013 ns â€“ 1932â€“1972: The Tuskegee Syphilis Experiment. In one of the most infamous examples of racist medical abuse, the U.S. Public Health Service conducted a 40-year study in Macon County, Alabama, observing the effects of syphilis on African American men. Hundreds of Black men with syphilis were deceived into thinking they were receiving â€œtreatment,â€ but were in fact deliberately left untreated â€“ even after penicillin was recognized as a cure â€“ so that doctors could document the long-term progression of the disease. Legal and Spiritual Reckonings
â€¢	0.000000019 ns â€“ 1948: Defining Genocide and Human Rights. Shocked by the Holocaust and colonial atrocities, the world community establishes the United Nations Genocide Convention (December 9, 1948)  . For the first time, genocide is defined in law â€“ not only as mass killing, but including â€œcausing serious bodily or mental harm to members of [a group],â€ â€œdeliberately inflicting conditions of life calculated to bring about the groupâ€™s destruction,â€ and measures to prevent births or remove children  . This broader definition encompasses many of the acts detailed above (from forced sterilizations to Jim Crow conditions). The Convention obligates states to prevent and punish genocide. Importantly, in 2006-2007 the International Court of Justice (ICJ) affirmed that the prohibition of genocide is a peremptory norm (jus cogens) â€“ a fundamental principle binding on all nations that no state can lawfully derogate . The ICJ also clarified that states can be held responsible not only for committing genocide, but for failing to prevent genocide when they had the capacity. These legal standards provide a framework to judge historical actions: e.g., the failure of the U.S. federal government to prevent lynchings and racial pogroms, or European powersâ€™ willful infliction of deadly living conditions in colonies, are arguably breaches of the Genocide Conventionâ€™s spirit if not its letter (which, for example, recognizes deliberately imposed famine as genocidal). The Genocide Convention thus offers a powerful language for survivors to articulate claims of historical justice on the world stage.
â€¢	0.000000020 ns â€“ 1951: â€œWe Charge Genocide.â€ On December 17, 1951, African American civil rights leaders William Patterson and Paul Robeson presented a petition to the United Nations titled We Charge Genocide: The Crime of Government Against the Negro People. Citing detailed evidence of lynchings (over 10,000 killed since 1865), legal discrimination, police brutality, and systemic inequalities, the petition argued that the U.S. was guilty of ongoing genocide against Black Americans . It invoked the new UN Convention, listing how each Article II criterion was met by U.S. conduct â€“ from killings (lynchings, executions) and serious bodily harm (beatings, medical neglect) to conditions of life (ghettos, denial of healthcare) intended to destroy the Black community. Though the petition did not lead to UN sanctions (it was during the Cold War and largely dismissed by U.S. officials), it was a landmark symbolic indictment. It foreshadowed later international judgments and galvanized civil rights activists to frame racism as not just a domestic issue but a human rights violation of the highest order. Seven decades later, in 2021, a new global tribunal would echo We Charge Genocideâ€™s findings almost verbatim , demonstrating the enduring resonance of that 1951 testimony.
â€¢	0.000000021 ns â€“ 2021: International Tribunal Verdict â€“ United States Guilty of Genocide. The Timeline Wheel: Sun at 12:00 to Witness at 6:00

The Timeline Wheel can be envisioned as a clock face, where 12:00 (the top) is marked by the Sun and 6:00 (bottom) by the Witness. Symbolically, the Sun at 12:00 represents the zenith of illumination and power â€“ at high noon, nothing is hidden. This evokes the historical height of imperial dominion, when colonial powers saw themselves as standing in the noonday sun, claiming divine sanction (the â€œsun never setsâ€ ethos) over vast empires. Indeed, the phrase â€œthe empire on which the sun never setsâ€ was first used for Spain and later Britain to boast that daylight was always shining on some part of their global realms . At 12:00 the Sunâ€™s position signals clear visibility and dominance; analogously, colonial empires at their apogee operated openly with supreme confidence, basking in wealth extracted from colonies.

By contrast, Witness at 6:00 occupies the opposite, nadir position â€“ symbolizing the perspective of those on the ground who bear witness in the shadows cast by the noonday sun. Historically, as imperial noon gave way to dusk, the oppressed peoples and truth-tellers emerged from darkness to testify to the crimes of empire. Witness at 6:00 signifies accountability: the phase when hidden atrocities come to light and narratives are inverted. It is the â€œsunsetâ€ of unquestioned imperial rule and the rise of scrutiny. For example, as the colonial era waned, investigations and tribunals (from the Nuremberg trials to Truth and Reconciliation Commissions) relied on eyewitness testimony to confront historical injustices  . Positionally, 6:00 is the bottom of the wheel â€“ suggesting a foundational vantage point of morality and memory that supports the whole. If the Sun at 12:00 denotes power proclaiming itself, the Witness at 6:00 denotes truth examining power.

In the context of the EV0L codex mythos, this timeline wheel implies a cyclical journey from the peak of illuminated authority to the depth of reflective witnessing. The Sun at 12:00 could also correspond to â€œhigh noonâ€ judgement in the codex â€“ a moment of full revelation â€“ whereas the Witness at 6:00 is like the midnight vigil, where every deed is accounted for. Together, they frame a 180Â° arc: the transition from midday (action) to midnight (reckoning) in historical cycles. Many colonial powers experienced this trajectory â€“ e.g. the British Empireâ€™s height in the 19th century (sun at zenith) followed by 20th-century decolonization and inquiries into colonial crimes (witness accounts). In sum, the Timeline Wheelâ€™s Sun-to-Witness path symbolizes the turning of time toward justice: what was done in the broad daylight of imperialism is eventually witnessed, recorded, and judged in the tribunal of history.
